<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains letters from the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Multilingual_support#Javanese" title="Help:Multilingual support">rendering support</a>, you may see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Replacement_character" title="Replacement character">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a class="external text" href="http://sites.google.com/site/jawaunicode/download" rel="nofollow">Javanese characters</a>.</td>
</tr>

<p><b>Javanese</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/dʒ/ 'j' in 'jam'">dʒ</span><span title="/ɑː/ 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span><span title="'z' in 'Zion'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> (<span lang="jv-java" style="font-size:16px; line-height:2.1em; word-wrap: break-word; font-family:'Tuladha Jejeg', 'Javanese Text', 'Noto Sans Javanese';" xml:lang="jv-java">ꦧꦱꦗꦮ</span>, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Javanese transliteration">basa Jawa</span></i>; <small>Javanese pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[bɔsɔ dʒɔwɔ]</a></span>) (colloquially known as <span lang="jv-java" style="font-size:16px; line-height:2.1em; word-wrap: break-word; font-family:'Tuladha Jejeg', 'Javanese Text', 'Noto Sans Javanese';" xml:lang="jv-java">ꦕꦫꦗꦮ</span>, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Javanese transliteration">cara Jawa</span></i>; <small>Javanese pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[tjɔrɔ dʒɔwɔ]</a></span>) is the language of the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese people</a> from the central and eastern parts of the island of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. There are also pockets of Javanese speakers in the northern coast of western Java. It is the native language of more than 98 million people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> (more than 42% of the total population of Indonesia).</p>
<p>Javanese is one of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>, but it is not particularly close to other languages and is difficult to classify. Its closest relatives are the neighbouring languages such as <a href="/wiki/Sundanese_language" title="Sundanese language">Sundanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madurese_language" title="Madurese language">Madurese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balinese_language" title="Balinese language">Balinese</a>. Most speakers of Javanese also speak <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>, the standardized form of <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> spoken in Indonesia, for official and commercial purposes as well as a means to communicate with non-Javanese-speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesian_people" title="Indonesian people">Indonesians</a>.</p>
<p>There are speakers of Javanese in <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> (concentrated in the states of <a href="/wiki/Selangor" title="Selangor">Selangor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johor" title="Johor">Johor</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>. Some people of Javanese descent in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> (the Dutch colony of <a href="/wiki/Surinam_(Dutch_colony)" title="Surinam (Dutch colony)">Surinam</a> until 1975) speak a <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole</a> descendant of the language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speakers">Speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:201px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aksara_Jawa_-_jawa.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="72" data-file-width="199" height="72" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Aksara_Jawa_-_jawa.png" width="199"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aksara_Jawa_-_jawa.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The word <i>Jawa</i> (<a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>) written in <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The language is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Special_Region_of_Yogyakarta" title="Special Region of Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a>, as well as on the north coast of <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>. It is also spoken elsewhere by the Javanese people in other provinces of Indonesia, which are numerous due to the government-sanctioned transmigration program in the late 20th century, including <a href="/wiki/Lampung" title="Lampung">Lampung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jambi" title="Jambi">Jambi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Sumatra" title="North Sumatra">North Sumatra</a> provinces. In Suriname, creolized Javanese is spoken among descendants of plantation migrants brought by the Dutch during the 19th century. In Madura, Bali, Lombok, and the Sunda region of West Java, it is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a>. It was the court language in <a href="/wiki/Palembang" title="Palembang">Palembang</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sumatra" title="South Sumatra">South Sumatra</a>, until the palace was sacked by the Dutch in the late 18th century.</p>
<p>Javanese is written with the <a href="/wiki/Javanese_Latin_alphabet" title="Javanese Latin alphabet">Latin script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pegon_alphabet" title="Pegon alphabet">Arabic script</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In the present day, the Latin script dominates writings, although the Javanese script is still taught as part of the compulsory Javanese language subject in elementary up to high school levels in Yogyakarta, Central and East Java.</p>
<p>Javanese is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">tenth largest language by native speakers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_languages_without_official_status" title="List of largest languages without official status">largest language without official status</a>. It is spoken or understood by approximately 100 million people. At least 45% of the total population of Indonesia are of Javanese descent or live in an area where Javanese is the dominant language. All seven Indonesian presidents since 1945 have been of Javanese descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> It is therefore not surprising that Javanese has had a deep influence on the development of Indonesian, the national language of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.</p>
<p>There are three main dialects of the modern language: Central Javanese, Eastern Javanese, and Western Javanese. These three dialects form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a> from northern <a href="/wiki/Banten" title="Banten">Banten</a> in the extreme west of Java to <a href="/wiki/Banyuwangi_Regency" title="Banyuwangi Regency">Banyuwangi Regency</a> in the eastern corner of the island. All Javanese dialects are more or less <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually intelligible</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The phonemes of Modern Standard Javanese as shown below.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concise_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-concise-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<center>
<table align="center" class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Front</th>
<th>Central</th>
<th>Back</th>
</tr>

<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">i</span></td>
<td>   </td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">u</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Half closed</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">e</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ə</span></td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">o</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Half open</th>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">(ɛ)</span></td>
<td> </td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">(ɔ)</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Open</th>
<td> </td>
<td><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">a</span></td>
<td> </td>
</tr>
</table>
</center>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_syllable" title="Closed syllable">closed syllables</a> the vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i u e o/</span> are pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɪ ʊ ɛ ɔ]</span> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concise_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-concise-7">[7]</a></sup> In open syllables, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e o/</span> are also <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛ ɔ]</span> when the following vowel is <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i u/</span> in an open syllable; otherwise they are <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ə/</span>, or identical (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/e...e/, /o...o/</span>). In the standard dialect of Surakarta, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> is pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔ]</span> in word-final open syllables, and in any open penultimate syllable before such an <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔ]</span>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<p>The Javanese "voiced" phonemes are not in fact <a href="/wiki/Voice_(phonetics)" title="Voice (phonetics)">voiced</a> but voiceless, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breathy_voice" title="Breathy voice">breathy voice</a> on the following vowel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concise_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-concise-7">[7]</a></sup> The relevant distinction in <a href="/wiki/Phonation" title="Phonation">phonation</a> of the plosives is described as <a href="/wiki/Stiff_voice" title="Stiff voice">stiff voice</a> versus <a href="/wiki/Slack_voice" title="Slack voice">slack voice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SOWL96_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SOWL96-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Javanese syllable can have the following <a href="/wiki/Syllable#Structure" title="Syllable">form</a>: CSVC, where C = <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a>, S = <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">sonorant</a> (<span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/, /r/, /l/, /w/</span>, or any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasal_stop" title="Nasal stop">nasal consonant</a>), and V = <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a>. As with other Austronesian languages, native Javanese roots consist of two syllables; words consisting of more than three syllables are broken up into groups of disyllabic words for pronunciation. In Modern Javanese, a disyllabic root is of the following type: nCsvVnCsvVC.</p>
<p>Apart from <a href="/wiki/Madurese_language" title="Madurese language">Madurese</a>, Javanese is the only language of Western Indonesia to possess a distinction between <a href="/wiki/Dental_consonant" title="Dental consonant">dental</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroflex" title="Retroflex">retroflex</a> phonemes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concise_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-concise-7">[7]</a></sup> The latter sounds are transcribed as "th" and "dh" in the modern Roman script, but previously by the use of an <a href="/wiki/Dot_(diacritic)" title="Dot (diacritic)">underdot</a>: "ṭ" and "ḍ". Some scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> take this to be an influence from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, but others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2009)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe it could be an independent development within the Austronesian super-family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Javanese, like other Austronesian languages, is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agglutinative" title="Agglutinative">agglutinative</a> language, where base words are modified through extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">Syntax</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Syntax">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Modern Javanese usually employs <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">SVO</a> word order. However, Old Javanese sometimes had <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93subject%E2%80%93object" title="Verb–subject–object">VSO</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Verb%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93subject" title="Verb–object–subject">VOS</a> word order. Even in Modern Javanese, archaic sentences using VSO structure can still be made.</p>
<p>Examples:</p>
<ul>
<li>Modern Javanese: "<i>Dhèwèké</i> (S) <i>teka</i> (V) <i>ing</i> (pp.) <i>karaton</i> (O)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li>Old Javanese: "<i>Teka</i> (V) <i>ta</i> (part.) <i>sira</i> (S) <i>ri</i> (pp.) <i>-ng</i> (def. art.) <i>kadhatwan</i> (O)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Both sentences mean: "He (S) comes (V) into (pp.) the (def. art.) palace (O)". In the Old Javanese sentence, the verb is placed at the beginning and is separated by the particle <i>ta</i> from the rest of the sentence. In Modern Javanese the definite article is lost, and definiteness is expressed by other means if necessary.</p>
<p>Verbs are not inflected for person or number. There is no grammatical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tense_(grammar)" title="Tense (grammar)">tense</a>; time is expressed by auxiliary words meaning "yesterday", "already", etc. There is a complex system of verb affixes to express differences of status in subject and object. However, in general the structure of Javanese sentences both Old and Modern can be described using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic%E2%80%93comment" title="Topic–comment">topic–comment model</a>, without having to refer to conventional grammatical categories. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic_(linguistics)" title="Topic (linguistics)">topic</a> is the head of the sentence; the comment is the modifier. So the example sentence has a simpler description: <i>Dhèwèké</i> = <i>topic</i>; <i>teka</i> = comment; <i>ing karaton</i> = setting.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Javanese has a rich and varied vocabulary, with many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">loanwords</a> supplementing those from the native Austronesian base. <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> has had a deep and lasting impact. The <i>Old Javanese–English Dictionary</i> contains approximately 25,500 entries, over 12,600 of which are borrowings from Sanskrit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Such a high number is no measure of usage, but it does suggest the extent to which the language adopted Sanskrit words for formal purposes. In a typical Old Javanese literary work about 25% of the vocabulary is from Sanskrit. Many Javanese personal names also have clearly recognisable Sanskrit roots.</p>
<p>Sanskrit words are still very much in use. Modern speakers may describe Old Javanese and Sanskrit words as <i><a href="/wiki/Kawi_language" title="Kawi language">kawi</a></i> (roughly meaning "literary"); but <i>kawi</i> words may also be from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> are influential as well; but none of these rivals the position of Sanskrit.</p>
<p>There are far fewer Arabic loanwords in Javanese than in Malay, and they are usually concerned with <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> religion. Nevertheless, some words have entered the basic vocabulary, such as <i>pikir</i> ("to think", from the Arabic <i>fikr</i>), <i>badan</i> ("body"), <i>mripat</i> ("eye", thought to be derived from the Arabic <i>ma'rifah</i>, meaning "knowledge" or "vision"). However, these Arabic words typically have native Austronesian or Sanskrit alternatives: <i>pikir</i> = <i>galih</i>, <i>idhĕp</i> (Austronesian) and <i>manah</i>, <i>cipta</i>, or <i>cita</i> (from Sanskrit); <i>badan</i> = <i>awak</i> (Austronesian) and <i>slira</i>, <i>sarira</i>, or <i>angga</i> (from Sanskrit); and <i>mripat</i> = <i>mata</i> (Austronesian) and <i>soca</i> or <i>netra</i> (from Sanskrit).</p>
<p>Dutch loanwords usually have the same form and meaning as in Indonesian, with a few exceptions:</p>
<center>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Javanese</th>
<th>Indonesian</th>
<th>Dutch</th>
<th>English</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>pit</i></td>
<td><i>sepeda</i></td>
<td><i>fiets</i></td>
<td>bicycle</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>pit montor</i></td>
<td><i>sepeda motor</i></td>
<td><i>motorfiets</i></td>
<td>motorcycle</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><i>sepur</i></td>
<td><i>kereta api</i></td>
<td><i>spoor</i>, i.e. (rail) track</td>
<td>train</td>
</tr>
</table>
</center>
<p>The word <i>sepur</i> also exists in Indonesian, but there it has preserved the <i>literal</i> Dutch meaning of "railway tracks", while the Javanese word follows Dutch <i>figurative</i> use, and "spoor" (lit. "rail") is used as <a href="/wiki/Metonymy" title="Metonymy">metonymy</a> for "trein" (lit. "train"). (Compare a similar metonymic use in English: "to travel by rail" may be used for "to travel by train".)</p>
<p>Malay was the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of the Indonesian archipelago before the proclamation of Indonesian independence in 1945; and Indonesian, which was based on Malay, is now the official language of Indonesia. As a consequence, there has been an influx of Malay and Indonesian vocabulary into Javanese. Many of these words are concerned with bureaucracy or politics.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Registers">Registers <span id="Registers"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Registers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="556" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Studioportret_met_de_echtgenote_van_kunstschilder_Raden_Saleh_en_een_bediende_Batavia_TMnr_60043601.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Javanese <a href="/wiki/Priyayi" title="Priyayi">noble lady</a> (left) would address her servant with one vocabulary, and be answered with another. (Studio portrait of painter <a href="/wiki/Raden_Saleh" title="Raden Saleh">Raden Saleh</a>'s wife and a servant, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_Indies" title="Dutch East Indies">colonial</a> <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Batavia</a>, 1860–1872.)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In common with other Austronesian languages, Javanese is spoken differently depending on the social context. In Austronesian there are often three distinct styles or <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">registers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Each employs its own vocabulary, grammatical rules, and even <a href="/wiki/Prosody_(linguistics)" title="Prosody (linguistics)">prosody</a>. In Javanese these styles are called:</p>
<ol>
<li><i>Ngoko</i> (<span lang="jv-java" style="font-size:16px; line-height:2.1em; word-wrap: break-word; font-family:'Tuladha Jejeg', 'Javanese Text', 'Noto Sans Javanese';" xml:lang="jv-java">ꦔꦺꦴꦏꦺꦴ</span>). Informal speech, used between friends and close relatives. It is also used by persons of higher status (such as elders, or bosses) addressing those of lower status (young people, or subordinates in the workplace).</li>
<li><i>Madya</i> (<span lang="jv-java" style="font-size:16px; line-height:2.1em; word-wrap: break-word; font-family:'Tuladha Jejeg', 'Javanese Text', 'Noto Sans Javanese';" xml:lang="jv-java">ꦩꦢꦾ</span>). Intermediate between <i>ngoko</i> and <i>krama</i>. Strangers on the street would use it, where status differences may be unknown and one wants to be neither too formal nor too informal. The term is from Sanskrit <i>madhya</i> ("middle").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-code_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-code-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Krama</i> (<span lang="jv-java" style="font-size:16px; line-height:2.1em; word-wrap: break-word; font-family:'Tuladha Jejeg', 'Javanese Text', 'Noto Sans Javanese';" xml:lang="jv-java">ꦏꦿꦩ</span>). The polite and formal style. It is used between those of the same status when they do not wish to be informal. It is used by persons of lower status to persons of higher status, such as young people to their elders, or subordinates to bosses; and it is the official style for public speeches, announcements, etc. The term is from Sanskrit <i>krama</i> ("in order").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-code_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-code-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>There are also "meta-style" <a href="/wiki/Honorific" title="Honorific">honorific</a> words, and their converse "humilifics". Speakers use "humble" words concerning themselves, but honorific words concerning anyone of greater age of higher social status. The humilific words are called <i>krama andhap</i>, while the honorifics are called <i>krama inggil</i>. Children typically use the <i>ngoko</i> style, but in talking to the parents they must be competent with both <i>krama inggil</i> and <i>krama andhap</i>. Some examples:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ngoko: <i>Aku arep mangan.</i> ("I want to eat.")</li>
<li>Madya: <i>Kula ajeng nedha.</i></li>
<li>Krama:
<ul>
<li>(Neutral) <i>Kula badhé nadhi.</i></li>
<li>(Humble) <i>Dalem badhé nedhi.</i></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The most polite word meaning "eat" is <i>dhahar</i>. But it is forbidden to use these most polite words for oneself, except when talking with someone of lower status; and in this case, ngoko style is used. Such most polite words are reserved for addressing people of higher status:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mixed usages
<ul>
<li>(honorific – addressing someone of high status) <i>Bapak kersa dhahar?</i> ("Do you want to eat?"; literally "Does father want to eat?")</li>
<li>(reply to a person of lower status, expressing speaker's superiority) <i>Iya, aku kersa dhahar.</i> ("Yes, I want to eat.")</li>
<li>(reply to a person of lower status, but without expressing superiority) <i>Iya, aku arep mangan.</i></li>
<li>(reply to a person of equal status) <i>Inggih, kula badhé nedha.</i></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The use of these different styles is complicated and requires thorough knowledge of Javanese culture, which adds to the difficulty of Javanese for foreigners. The full system is not usually mastered by most Javanese themselves, who might use only the <i>ngoko</i> and a rudimentary form of the <i>krama</i>. People who can correctly use the different styles are held in high esteem.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects_of_modern_Javanese">Dialects of modern Javanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dialects of modern Javanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="533" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg/220px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg/330px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg/440px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Portret_van_Pakoe_Boewono_X_soesoehoenan_van_Soerakarta_TMnr_10001886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pakubuwono" title="Pakubuwono">Susuhunan Pakubuwono X of Surakarta</a>. <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a> has been a center of Javanese culture, and its dialect is regarded as the most "refined".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are three main groups of Javanese <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a>, based on sub-regions: Western Javanese, Central Javanese, and Eastern Javanese. The differences are primarily in pronunciation, but with vocabulary differences also. Javanese dialects are all mutually intelligible.</p>
<p><b>Central Javanese</b> (<i>Jawa Tengahan</i>) is founded on the speech of <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and to a lesser extent of <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>. It is considered the most "refined" of the regional variants, and serves as a model for the standard language. Those two cities are the seats of four Javanese principalities (heirs to the Mataram Sultanate) that once dominated the whole of Java and beyond. This variant is used throughout <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_Region_of_Yogyakarta" title="Special Region of Yogyakarta">Special Region of Yogyakarta</a>, and there are many lower-level dialects such as <i>Muria</i> and <i>Semarangan</i>, as well as <i>Surakarta</i> and <i>Yogyakarta</i> themselves. The variations in Central Java are said to be so plentiful that almost every administrative region (or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regency_(Indonesia)" title="Regency (Indonesia)">kabupatèn</a></i>) has its own local slang; but those minor dialects are not seen as distinct by most Javanese speakers.</p>
<p>Central Javanese is also used in the western part of East Java province. For example, Javanese spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Madiun" title="Madiun">Madiun</a> region (along with Javanese spoken in <a href="/wiki/Blitar" title="Blitar">Blitar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponorogo" title="Ponorogo">Ponorogo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacitan" title="Pacitan">Pacitan</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tulungagung" title="Tulungagung">Tulungagung</a>, and central parts of <a href="/wiki/Kediri,_East_Java" title="Kediri, East Java">Kediri</a>) bears a strong influence of Surakarta Javanese.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>Mataraman dialect</b> / <b>Standard dialect</b> is spoken commonly in <a href="/wiki/Special_Region_of_Yogyakarta" title="Special Region of Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Klaten_Regency" title="Klaten Regency">Klaten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karanganyar_Regency" title="Karanganyar Regency">Karanganyar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wonogiri_Regency" title="Wonogiri Regency">Wonogiri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sukoharjo_Regency" title="Sukoharjo Regency">Sukoharjo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sragen_Regency" title="Sragen Regency">Sragen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boyolali_Regency" title="Boyolali Regency">Boyolali</a>.</li>
<li><b>Pekalongan dialect</b> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Pekalongan" title="Pekalongan">Pekalongan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pekalongan_Regency" title="Pekalongan Regency">Pekalongan regency</a>, and also in <a href="/wiki/Pemalang_Regency" title="Pemalang Regency">Pemalang</a>.</li>
<li><b>Kedu dialect</b> is spoken in the former <a href="/wiki/Kedu_Residency" title="Kedu Residency">Kedu residency</a>, including: <a href="/wiki/Temanggung_Regency" title="Temanggung Regency">Temanggung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kebumen_Regency" title="Kebumen Regency">Kebumen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magelang_Regency" title="Magelang Regency">Magelang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wonosobo_Regency" title="Wonosobo Regency">Wonosobo</a>.</li>
<li><b>Bagelen dialect</b> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Purworejo_Regency" title="Purworejo Regency">Purworejo</a>.</li>
<li><b>Semarang dialect</b> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Semarang" title="Semarang">Semarang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semarang_Regency" title="Semarang Regency">Semarang regency</a>, and also <a href="/wiki/Salatiga" title="Salatiga">Salatiga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demak_Regency" title="Demak Regency">Demak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kendal_Regency" title="Kendal Regency">Kendal</a>.</li>
<li><b>Eastern North-Coast dialect</b>, or <i>dhialèk <a href="/wiki/Mount_Muria" title="Mount Muria">Muria</a></i>, is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Jepara_Regency" title="Jepara Regency">Jepara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rembang_Regency" title="Rembang Regency">Rembang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kudus_Regency" title="Kudus Regency">Kudus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pati_Regency" title="Pati Regency">Pati</a>, and also in <a href="/wiki/Tuban_Regency" title="Tuban Regency">Tuban</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bojonegoro_Regency" title="Bojonegoro Regency">Bojonegoro</a>.</li>
<li><b>Blora dialect</b> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Blora_Regency" title="Blora Regency">Blora</a>, the eastern part of <a href="/wiki/Grobogan_Regency" title="Grobogan Regency">Grobogan</a>, and the western part of <a href="/wiki/Ngawi_Regency" title="Ngawi Regency">Ngawi</a>.</li>
<li><b>Madiunan dialect</b> is spoken mainly in western part of <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a> province, including <a href="/wiki/Madiun" title="Madiun">Madiun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blitar" title="Blitar">Blitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ngawi_Regency" title="Ngawi Regency">Ngawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacitan_Regency" title="Pacitan Regency">Pacitan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ponorogo_Regency" title="Ponorogo Regency">Ponorogo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magetan_Regency" title="Magetan Regency">Magetan</a>.</li>
</ol>
<p><b>Western Javanese</b> (<i>Jawa Kulonan</i>), spoken in the western part of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a> province and throughout the <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a> province (particularly on the north coast), includes dialects that are distinct for their <a href="/wiki/Sundanese_language" title="Sundanese language">Sundanese</a> influences. It retains many archaic words.</p>
<ol>
<li><b>North <a href="/wiki/Banten" title="Banten">Banten</a> dialect</b> (<i>Jawa Serang</i>) is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Serang_Regency" title="Serang Regency">Serang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilegon" title="Cilegon">Cilegon</a>, and the western part of <a href="/wiki/Tangerang_Regency" title="Tangerang Regency">Tangerang</a> regency.</li>
<li><b>Cirebon dialect</b> (<i>Cirebonan</i> or <i>Basa Cerbon</i>) is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Cirebon_Regency" title="Cirebon Regency">Cirebon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Losari,_Brebes" title="Losari, Brebes">Losari</a>. <b>Indramayu dialect</b> (or <i>Dermayon</i>), which is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Indramayu_Regency" title="Indramayu Regency">Indramayu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karawang_Regency" title="Karawang Regency">Karawang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subang_Regency" title="Subang Regency">Subang</a>, is sometimes included<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> in Cirebonan.</li>
<li><b>Tegal dialect</b>, known as <i>Tegalan</i> or <i>Dhialèk Pantura</i> (North-Coast dialect), is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Tegal_Regency" title="Tegal Regency">Tegal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brebes_Regency" title="Brebes Regency">Brebes</a>, and the western part of <a href="/wiki/Pemalang_Regency" title="Pemalang Regency">Pemalang</a> regency.</li>
<li><b>Banyumas dialect</b>, known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyumasan_language" title="Banyumasan language">Banyumasan</a></i>, is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Banyumas_Regency" title="Banyumas Regency">Banyumas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cilacap_Regency" title="Cilacap Regency">Cilacap</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purbalingga_Regency" title="Purbalingga Regency">Purbalingga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Banjarnegara_Regency" title="Banjarnegara Regency">Banjarnegara</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bumiayu,_Brebes" title="Bumiayu, Brebes">Bumiayu</a>.</li>
</ol>
<p>These Western Javanese dialects are sometimes referred to as <i>basa ngapak-apak</i> by other Javanese.</p>
<p><b>Eastern Javanese</b> (<i>Jawa Wétanan</i>) speakers range from the eastern banks of <a href="/wiki/Brantas_River" title="Brantas River">Brantas River</a> in Kertosono, and from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nganjuk" title="Nganjuk">Nganjuk</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyuwangi" title="Banyuwangi">Banyuwangi</a>, comprising the majority of the <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a> province excluding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madura" title="Madura">Madura</a> island. However, the variant has been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Madurese_language" title="Madurese language">Madurese</a>, and is sometimes referred to<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> as <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabayan</a> speech.</p>
<p>The most outlying Eastern Javanese dialect is spoken in Balambangan (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyuwangi" title="Banyuwangi">Banyuwangi</a>). It is generally known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osing_language" title="Osing language">Basa Using</a></i>. <i>Osing</i>, a local negation word, is a cognate of <i>tusing</i> in <a href="/wiki/Balinese_language" title="Balinese language">Balinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><b>Arekan dialect</b> is commonly spoken in <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malang_Regency" title="Malang Regency">Malang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gresik_Regency" title="Gresik Regency">Gresik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mojokerto_Regency" title="Mojokerto Regency">Mojokerto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pasuruan_Regency" title="Pasuruan Regency">Pasuruan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumajang_Regency" title="Lumajang Regency">Lumajang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamongan_Regency" title="Lamongan Regency">Lamongan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sidoarjo_Regency" title="Sidoarjo Regency">Sidoarjo</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Madurese_people" title="Madurese people">Madurese</a> people also use this dialect as their second language.</li>
<li><b>Jombang dialect</b></li>
<li><b>Tengger dialect</b> used by <a href="/wiki/Tenggerese" title="Tenggerese">Tenggerese</a> people, which is centered in thirty villages in the isolated Tengger mountains (<a href="/wiki/Mount_Bromo" title="Mount Bromo">Mount Bromo</a>) within the <a href="/wiki/Bromo_Tengger_Semeru_National_Park" title="Bromo Tengger Semeru National Park">Bromo Tengger Semeru National Park</a> in East-Central <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Osing_dialect" title="Osing dialect">Osing dialect</a></b> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Banyuwangi_Regency" title="Banyuwangi Regency">Banyuwangi</a>.</li>
</ol>
<p><b>Surinamese-Javanese</b> is mainly based on Central Javanese, especially from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kedu_residency" title="Kedu residency">Kedu residency</a>. The number of speakers of Suriname-Javanese in Suriname is estimated at 60.000 (2012).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taalunieversum.org_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-taalunieversum.org-17">[17]</a></sup> Most Surinamese-Javanese are bi- or trilingual. According to the 2004 census, Surinamese-Javanese was the first or second language in 11 percent of households. In a 2012 study of multilingualism in Surinamese education by the Dutch Language Union,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taalunieversum.org_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-taalunieversum.org-17">[17]</a></sup> 3497 out of 22.643 (15 percent) pupils in primary education indicated Surinamese-Javanese as a language spoken at home. Most of them were living in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wanica" title="Wanica">Wanica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paramaribo_District" title="Paramaribo District">Paramaribo</a> districts.</p>
<p>Not all immigrants from Indonesia to Suriname were speakers of Javanese. Immigration records show that 90 percent of immigrants were Javanese, with 5 percent Sundanese, 0,5 percent Madurese and 2,5 percent from Batavia. The ethnic composition of this last group was not determinable. Probably Sundanese, Madurese or Malay speaking immigrants were forced to learn Javanese during their stay in Suriname to adapt. In view of the language policies in Netherlands Indies at the time of immigration, it is unlikely the immigrants had knowledge of the Dutch language prior to immigration to Suriname. Dutch today is the official language of Suriname.</p>
<p>Surinamese Javanese is somewhat different from Indonesian Javanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbnl.org_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-dbnl.org-18">[18]</a></sup> In Surinamese-Javanese there is a difference between formal and informal speech. Surinamese-Javanese took many loanwords from languages like Dutch, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sranantongo" title="Sranantongo">Sranantongo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarnami" title="Sarnami">Sarnami</a> and Bahasa Indonesia. The influence of the latter language, which is not spoken in Suriname, can be attributed to the Indonesian embassy and Islamic teachers from Indonesia. Indonesian movies are popular, and usually shown without subtitles on Surinamese-Javanese television channels.</p>
<center>

<p>In 1986, the Surinamese government adopted an official spelling for Surinamese-Javanese. It is seldom used as a written language, however.</p>
<p>In the 2012 survey, pupils who indicated Surinamese-Javanese as a language spoken at home, reported Dutch (97,9 percent) and Sranantongo (76,9 percent) also being spoken in the household.</p>
<p>Surinamese-Javanese speaking pupils report high proficiency in speaking and understanding, but very low literacy in the language. They report a low preference for the language in interaction with family members, including their parents, with the exception of their grandparents. Pupils where Surinamese-Javanese is spoken at tend at home to speak Dutch (77 percent) rather than Surinamese-Javanese (12 percent).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetic_differences">Phonetic differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Phonetic differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Phoneme /i/ at closed <a href="/wiki/Ultima_(linguistics)" title="Ultima (linguistics)">ultima</a> is pronounced as [ɪ] in Central Javanese (Surakarta–Yogyakarta dialect), as [i] in Western Javanese (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyumasan_language" title="Banyumasan language">Banyumasan</a> dialect), and as [ɛ] in Eastern Javanese.</p>
<p>Phoneme /u/ at closed ultima is pronounced as [ʊ] in Central Javanese, as [u] in Western Javanese, and as [ɔ] in Eastern Javanese.</p>
<p>Phoneme /a/ at closed ultima in Central Javanese is pronounced as [a] and at open ultima as [ɔ]. Regardless of position, it tends toward [a] in Western Javanese and as [ɔ] in Eastern Javanese.</p>
<p>Western Javanese tend to glottalize every last vowel of a word as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euphony" title="Euphony">euphony</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, e.g.: <i>Ana apa?</i> [ana apaʔ] "What happened?", <i>Aja kaya kuwè!</i> [adʒak kajak kuɛʔ] "Don't be like that!".</p>

<p>Final consonant devoicing occurs in the standard Central Javanese dialect, but not in Banyumasan. For example, egg is pronounced [ɛnɖɔʔ] in standard Central Javanese, but [ɛnɖɔg] in Banyumasan. The latter is closer to <a href="/wiki/Old_Javanese" title="Old Javanese">Old Javanese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lingweb.eva.mpg.de_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-lingweb.eva.mpg.de-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary_differences">Vocabulary differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Vocabulary differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The vocabulary of standard Javanese is enriched by dialectal words. For example, to get the meaning of "you", Western Javanese speakers say <i>rika</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/rikaʔ/</span>, Eastern Javanese use <i>kon</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kɔn/</span> or <i>koen</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kɔən/</span>, and Central Javanese speakers say <i>kowé</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kowe/</span>. Another example is the expression of "how": the Tegal dialect of Western Javanese uses <i>kepribèn</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kəpriben/</span>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyumasan_language" title="Banyumasan language">Banyumasan</a> dialect of Western Javanese employs <i>kepriwé</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kəpriwe/</span> or <i>kepriwèn</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kəpriwen/</span>, Eastern Javanese speakers say <i>ya' apa</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/jɔʔ ɔpɔ/</span> – originally meaning "like what" (<i>kaya apa</i> in standard Javanese) or <i>kepiyé</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kəpije/</span> – and Central Javanese speakers say <i>piye</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/pije/</span> or <i>kepriyé</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/kəprije/</span>.</p>
<center>

<p>The Madiun–Kediri dialect has some idiosyncratic vocabulary, such as <i>panggah</i> 'still' (standard Javanese: <i>pancet</i>), <i>lagèk</i> 'progressive modal' (standard Javanese: <i>lagi</i>), and emphatic particles <i>nda</i>, <i>pèh</i>, and <i>lé</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MadiunKediri_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-MadiunKediri-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A preliminary general classification of Javanese dialects given by the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Evolutionary_Anthropology" title="Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology">Max Planck Institute for Evolutionary Anthropology</a>'s Department of Linguistics is as follows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Pesisir (Pemalang) and Tengger are considered to be among the most conservative dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pemalang_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pemalang-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Banten" title="Banten">Banten</a>, Pesisir Lor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banyumas" title="Banyumas">Banyumas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tenggerese" title="Tenggerese">Tengger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Osing_dialect" title="Osing dialect">Osing</a> dialects do not have the vowel raising and vowel harmony features that are innovations of the "standard" Solo and Yogyakarta dialects.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Javanese">Old Javanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Old Javanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Javanese_language" title="Old Javanese language">Old Javanese language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="2246" height="26" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg/220px-Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg/330px-Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg/440px-Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palmleaf_of_Kakawin_Sutasoma_from_Java01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Palm leaf manuscript of <a href="/wiki/Kakawin_Sutasoma" title="Kakawin Sutasoma">Kakawin Sutasoma</a>, a 14th-century Javanese poem.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While evidence of writing in Java dates to the Sanskrit "<i>Tarumanegara</i> inscription" of 450 AD, the oldest example written entirely in Javanese, called the "Sukabumi inscription", is dated 25 March 804. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inscription" title="Inscription">inscription</a>, located in the district of Pare in the Kediri regency of East Java, is actually a copy of an original that is about 120 years older; only this copy has been preserved. Its contents concern the construction of a dam for an irrigation canal near the river Śrī Hariñjing (present-day Srinjing). This inscription is the last known of its kind to be written using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pallava_script" title="Pallava script">Pallava script</a>; all extant subsequent examples are written using <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a>.</p>
<p>The 8th and 9th centuries are marked by the emergence of the Javanese literary tradition – with <i>Sang Hyang Kamahayanikan</i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> treatise; and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kakawin_R%C4%81m%C3%A2ya%E1%B9%87a" title="Kakawin Rāmâyaṇa">Kakawin Rāmâyaṇa</a></i>, a Javanese rendering in Indian metres of the <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavist</a> Sanskrit epic <i>Rāmāyaṇa</i>.</p>
<p>Although Javanese as a written language appeared considerably later than Malay (extant in the 7th century), the Javanese literary tradition has been continuous from its inception. The oldest works – such as the <i>Kakawin Rāmâyaṇa</i> and a Javanese rendering of the Indian <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mah%C4%81bh%C4%81rata" title="Mahābhārata">Mahābhārata</a></i> epic – are studied assiduously today.</p>
<p>The expansion of Javanese culture, including Javanese script and language, began in 1293 with the eastward push of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> East-Javanese Empire <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a>, toward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madura" title="Madura">Madura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>. The Javanese campaign in Bali in 1363 had a deep and lasting impact, and Javanese replaced Balinese as the language of administration and literature. Though the Balinese people preserved much of the older literature of Java and even created their own in Javanese idioms, Balinese ceased to be written until a 19th-century restoration.</p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kawi_language" title="Kawi language">Kawi language</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Javanese">Middle Javanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Middle Javanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Majapahit Empire saw the rise of Middle Javanese as effectively a new language, intermediate between Old and New Javanese, though Middle Javanese is similar enough to New Javanese to be understood by anyone who is well acquainted with current literary Javanese.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1980" data-file-width="1296" height="336" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg/220px-Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg/330px-Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg/440px-Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prasasti_Masjid_Sholihin01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A New Javanese inscription in Sholihin Mosque, <a href="/wiki/Surakarta" title="Surakarta">Surakarta</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Majapahit Empire fell due to internal disturbances in the Paregreg civil war, thought to have occurred in 1405 and 1406, and attacks by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> forces of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Demak" title="Sultanate of Demak">Sultanate of Demak</a> on the north coast of Java. There is a Javanese <a href="/wiki/Chronogram" title="Chronogram">chronogram</a> concerning the fall that reads "<i>sirna ilang krĕtaning bumi</i>" ("vanished and gone was the prosperity of the World"), indicating the date 1478 AD, giving rise to a popular belief that Majapahit collapsed in 1478, though it may have lasted into the 16th century. This was the last Hindu Javanese empire.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Javanese">New Javanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: New Javanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 16th century a new era in Javanese history began with the rise of the Islamic Central Javanese <a href="/wiki/Mataram_Sultanate" title="Mataram Sultanate">Mataram Sultanate</a>, originally a vassal state of Majapahit. Ironically, the Mataram Empire rose as an Islamic kingdom that sought revenge for the demise of the Hindu Majapahit Empire by first crushing <a href="/wiki/Demak_Sultanate" title="Demak Sultanate">Demak</a>, the first Javanese Islamic kingdom.</p>
<p>Javanese culture spread westward as Mataram conquered many previously Sundanese areas in western parts of Java; and Javanese became the dominant language in more than a third of this area. As with Balinese, the Sundanese language ceased to be written until the 19th century. In the meantime it was heavily influenced by Javanese, and some 40% of Sundanese vocabulary is believed to have been derived from Javanese.</p>
<p>Though Islamic in name, the Mataram II empire preserved many elements of the older culture, incorporating them into the newly adopted religion. This is why Javanese script is still in use, as opposed to the writing of Old <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> for example. After the Malays were converted, they dropped their form of indigenous writing and changed to a form of the "script of the Divine", the Arabic script.</p>
<p>In addition to the rise of Islam, the 16th century saw the emergence of the New Javanese language. The first Islamic documents in Javanese were already written in New Javanese, although still in antiquated idioms and with numerous Arabic loanwords. This is to be expected: these early New Javanese documents are Islamic treatises.</p>
<p>Later, intensive contacts with the Dutch and with other Indonesians gave rise to a simplified form of Javanese and influx of foreign loanwords.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Javanese">Modern Javanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Modern Javanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (October 2015)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> dub the spoken form of Javanese in the 20th century Modern Javanese, although it is essentially still the same language as New Javanese.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Javanese_script">Javanese script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Javanese script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Javanese_script01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Javanese_script01.jpg/220px-Javanese_script01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Javanese_script01.jpg/330px-Javanese_script01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Javanese_script01.jpg/440px-Javanese_script01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Javanese_script01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A modern bilingual text in Portuguese and Javanese in <a href="/wiki/Yogyakarta" title="Yogyakarta">Yogyakarta</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a></div>
<p>Javanese has been traditionally written with <a href="/wiki/Javanese_script" title="Javanese script">Javanese script</a>. Javanese and the related <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balinese_script" title="Balinese script">Balinese script</a> are modern variants of the old <a href="/wiki/Kawi_script" title="Kawi script">Kawi script</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmic_script" title="Brahmic script">Brahmic script</a> introduced to Java along with Hinduism and Buddhism. Kawi is first attested in a legal document from 804 AD. It was widely used in literature and translations from Sanskrit from the 10th century; by the 17th, the script is identified as <i>carakan</i>.</p>
<p>The Javanese script is an abugida. Each of the twenty letter represents a syllable with a consonant (or a "zero consonant") and the inherent vowel 'a' that is pronounced as /ɔ/ in open position. Various diacritics placed around the letter indicate a different vowel than [ɔ], a final consonant, or a foreign pronunciation.</p>
<p>Letters have subscript forms used to transcribe consonant clusters, though the shape are relatively straightforward, and not as distinct as conjunct forms of <a href="/wiki/Devanagari" title="Devanagari">Devanagari</a>. Some letters are only present in <a href="/wiki/Old_Javanese" title="Old Javanese">old Javanese</a> and became obsolete in modern Javanese. Some of these letter became "capital" forms used in proper names. Punctuation includes a comma; period; a mark that covers the colon, quotations, and indicates numerals; and marks to introduce a chapter, poem, song, or letter.</p>
<p>However, Javanese can also be written with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">Arabic script</a> and today generally uses <a href="/wiki/Javanese_Latin_alphabet" title="Javanese Latin alphabet">Latin script</a> instead of Javanese script for practical purposes. A Latin <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> based on Dutch was introduced in 1926, revised in 1972–1973; it has largely supplanted the carakan. The current Latin-based forms:</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_distribution_of_Javanese_speakers">Demographic distribution of Javanese speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Demographic distribution of Javanese speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese people</a></div>
<p>Javanese is spoken throughout Indonesia, neighboring <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> countries, the Netherlands, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, and other countries. The largest populations of speakers are found in the six provinces of Java itself, and in the neighboring Sumatran province of <a href="/wiki/Lampung" title="Lampung">Lampung</a>.</p>

</center>
<p>According to the 1980 census, Javanese was used daily in approximately 43% of Indonesian households. By this reckoning there were well over 60 million Javanese speakers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> from a national population of 147,490,298.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raden_Segara_(Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890)_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="748" data-file-width="632" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Raden_Segara_%28Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890%29_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raden_Segara_(Madurese_in_Javanese_script-published_in_1890)_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Madurese_language" title="Madurese language">Madurese</a> in Javanese script.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Banten, the descendants of the Central Javanese conquerors who founded the Islamic Sultanate there in the 16th century still speak an archaic form of Javanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The rest of the population mainly speaks Sundanese and Indonesian, since this province borders directly on Jakarta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>At least one third of the population of <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a> are of Javanese descent, so they speak Javanese or have knowledge of it. In the province of <a href="/wiki/West_Java" title="West Java">West Java</a>, many people speak Javanese, especially those living in the areas bordering <a href="/wiki/Central_Java" title="Central Java">Central Java</a>, the cultural homeland of the Javanese.</p>
<p>Almost a quarter of the population of <a href="/wiki/East_Java" title="East Java">East Java</a> province are <a href="/wiki/Madurese_people" title="Madurese people">Madurese</a> (mostly on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madura" title="Madura">Isle of Madura</a>); many Madurese have some knowledge of colloquial Javanese. Since the 19th century, Madurese was also written in the Javanese script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The original inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/Lampung" title="Lampung">Lampung</a>, the Lampungese, make up only 15% of the provincial population. The rest are the so-called "transmigrants", settlers from other parts of Indonesia, many as a result of past government <a href="/wiki/Transmigration_program" title="Transmigration program">transmigration programs</a>. Most of these transmigrants are Javanese who have settled there since the 19th century.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a> (the former <a href="/wiki/Surinam_(Dutch_colony)" title="Surinam (Dutch colony)">Dutch colony of Surinam</a>), South America, approximately 15% of the population of some 500,000 are of Javanese descent, among whom 75,000 speak Javanese. A local variant evolved: the <i>Tyoro Jowo-Suriname</i> or <i>Suriname Javanese</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Javanese_today">Javanese today</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Javanese_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Javanese today">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Java_languages.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="681" height="77" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Java_languages.JPG/220px-Java_languages.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Java_languages.JPG/330px-Java_languages.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Java_languages.JPG/440px-Java_languages.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Java_languages.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution map of languages spoken in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madura" title="Madura">Madura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Javanese is not a national language, it has recognized status as a <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional language</a> in the three Indonesian provinces with the biggest concentrations of Javanese people: Central Java, Yogyakarta, and East Java. Javanese is taught at schools and is used in some <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a>, both electronically and in print. There is, however, no longer a daily newspaper in Javanese. Javanese language magazines include <i>Panjebar Semangat</i>, <i>Jaka Lodhang</i>, <i>Jaya Baya</i>, <i>Damar Jati</i>, and <i>Mekar Sari</i>.</p>
<p>Since 2003, an East Java local television station (<a href="/wiki/JTV_(Indonesia)" title="JTV (Indonesia)">JTV</a>) has broadcast some of its programmes in Surabayan dialect, including <i>Pojok kampung</i> (news), <i>Kuis RT/RW</i>, and <i>Pojok Perkoro</i> (a crime programme). In later broadcasts, JTV offers programmes in Central Javanese dialect (that they call <i>basa kulonan</i>, "the western language") and Madurese.</p>
<p>In 2005 a new Javanese language magazine, <i>Damar Jati</i>, appeared. It is not published in the Javanese heartlands, but in Jakarta.</p>

J. Habibie">Habibie</a> has a father of <a href="/wiki/Gorontalo" title="Gorontalo">Gorontalo</a> descent and a Javanese mother, while <a href="/wiki/Megawati_Sukarnoputri" title="Megawati Sukarnoputri">Megawati</a> is <a href="/wiki/Sukarno" title="Sukarno">Sukarno</a>'s daughter through his wife, who is from <a href="/wiki/Bengkulu" title="Bengkulu">Bengkulu</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-concise-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-concise_7-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-concise_7-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-concise_7-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-concise_7-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Brown, Keith; Ogilvie, Sarah (2008). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=F2SRqDzB50wC&amp;pg=PA560&amp;dq=%22javanese+phonology%22&amp;cd=7#v=onepage&amp;q=%22javanese%20phonology%22&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow"><i>Concise encyclopedia of languages of the world</i></a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2010-05-24</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJavanese+language&amp;rft.aufirst=Keith&amp;rft.aulast=Brown&amp;rft.au=Ogilvie%2C+Sarah&amp;rft.btitle=Concise+encyclopedia+of+languages+of+the+world&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DF2SRqDzB50wC%26pg%3DPA560%26dq%3D%2522javanese%2Bphonology%2522%26cd%3D7%23v%3Donepage%26q%3D%2522javanese%2520phonology%2522%26f%3Dfalse&amp;rft.isbn=9780080877747&amp;rft.pages=560&amp;rft.pub=Elsevier&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span> Madurese also possesses aspirated phonemes, including at least one aspirated retroflex phoneme.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-SOWL96-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-SOWL96_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book" id="CITEREFLadefogedMaddieson1996"><a href="/wiki/Peter_Ladefoged" title="Peter Ladefoged">Ladefoged, Peter</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ian_Maddieson" title="Ian Maddieson">Maddieson, Ian</a> (1996). <i>The Sounds of the World's Languages</i>. <li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A sibilant before a retroflex stop in Sanskrit loanwords is pronounced as a retroflex sibilant, whereas in modern Indian languages it is pronounced as a palatal sibilant. Though <a href="/wiki/Acehnese_language" title="Acehnese language">Acehnese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balinese_language" title="Balinese language">Balinese</a> also possess a retroflex voiceless stop, this is merely an allophone of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Piwulang Basa Jawa Pepak, S.B. Pramono, hal 148, Babad Hanacaraka, 2013</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Old Javanese spelling is modified to suit Modern Javanese spelling.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The distribution of persons living in Javanese-speaking households in East Java and Lampung requires clarification. For East Java, daily-language percentages are as follows: 74.5 Javanese, 23.0 Madurese, and 2.2 Indonesian. For Lampung, the official percentages are 62.4 Javanese, 16.4 Lampungese and other languages, 10.5 Sundanese, and 9.4 Indonesian. The figures are somewhat outdated for some regions, especially Jakarta; but they remain more or less stable for the rest of Java. In Jakarta the number of Javanese has increased tenfold in the last 25 years. On the other hand, because of the <a href="/wiki/Free_Aceh_Movement" title="Free Aceh Movement">conflict</a> the number of Javanese in <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a> might have decreased. It is also relevant that <a href="/wiki/Banten" title="Banten">Banten</a> has separated from West Java province in 2000.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pigeaud (1967:10-11).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Many commuters to Jakarta live in the suburbs in Banten, among them also Javanese speakers. Their exact number is unknown.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Unfortunately, the aspirated phonemes of Madurese are not reproduced in writing. The 19th-century scribes apparently overlooked the fact that Javanese script does possess the required characters.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bartje S. Setrowidjojo and Ruben T. Setrowidjojo <i>Het Surinaams-Javaans = Tyoro Jowo-Suriname</i>, Den Haag: Suara Jawa, 1994, <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9080212512">ISBN 90-802125-1-2</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Piwulang Basa Jawa Pepak, S.B. Pramono, hal 148, 2013</span></li>
